  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_6bcf430e-d83f-4f63-b4da-b3a26e976a1f'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();

      initializeSiwa();

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer14040565451871337570">Over five years in the writing, The Dovekeepers is Alice Hoffman's most ambitious and mesmerizing novel, a tour de force of imagination and research, set in ancient Israel.<br/><br/>In 70 C.E., nine hundred Jews held out for months against armies of Romans on Masada, a mountain in the Judean desert. According to the ancient historian Josephus, two women and five children survived. B</span>
  <span id="freeText14040565451871337570" style="display:none">Over five years in the writing, The Dovekeepers is Alice Hoffman's most ambitious and mesmerizing novel, a tour de force of imagination and research, set in ancient Israel.<br /><br />In 70 C.E., nine hundred Jews held out for months against armies of Romans on Masada, a mountain in the Judean desert. According to the ancient historian Josephus, two women and five children survived. Based on this tragic and iconic event, Hoffman's novel is a spellbinding tale of four extraordinarily bold, resourceful, and sensuous women, each of whom has come to Masada by a different path. Yael's mother died in childbirth, and her father, an expert assassin, never forgave her for that death. Revka, a village baker's wife, watched the horrifically brutal murder of her daughter by Roman soldiers; she brings to Masada her young grandsons, rendered mute by what they have witnessed. Aziza is a warrior's daughter, raised as a boy, a fearless rider and an expert marksman who finds passion with a fellow soldier. Shirah, born in Alexandria, is wise in the ways of ancient magic and medicine, a woman with uncanny insight and power. <br /><br />The lives of these four complex and fiercely independent women intersect in the desperate days of the siege. All are dovekeepers, and all are also keeping secrets - about who they are, where they come from, who fathered them, and whom they love.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14040565451871337570" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_243404133" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/243404133" />
    <a title="Kim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/520753-kim"><img alt="Kim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1402541317p2/520753.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/243404133?book_show_action=true">Dec 05, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kim" href="/user/show/520753-kim">Kim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=mmxi">mmxi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/520753-kim?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer243404133" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13858510911918326645">There are many reasons why I wouldn’t like this book:<br/><br/>1. I hate feeling dumb.<br/><br/>2. It’s set in Ancient Israel, 70 C.E. to be exact, and the fact that I had to ask what C.E. meant --being a child of B.C and A.D --did not go over well (Refer to #1)<br/><br/>3. It’s set in Ancient Israel and I, shamefully, have absolutely no clue what happened back then. I mean… besides the Last Temptation of Christ and bible stories that I kind of sort of remember. (#1)<br/><br/>4. The author also wrote Practical Magic, which I have not </span>
  <span id="freeText13858510911918326645" style="display:none">There are many reasons why I wouldn’t like this book:<br /><br />1. I hate feeling dumb.<br /><br />2. It’s set in Ancient Israel, 70 C.E. to be exact, and the fact that I had to ask what C.E. meant --being a child of B.C and A.D --did not go over well (Refer to #1)<br /><br />3. It’s set in Ancient Israel and I, shamefully, have absolutely no clue what happened back then. I mean… besides the Last Temptation of Christ and bible stories that I kind of sort of remember. (#1)<br /><br />4. The author also wrote Practical Magic, which I have not read but I have seen the movie and besides it being pretty damn awful, I got a serious complex having to look at Nicole Kidman and Sandra Bullock slink around in little black dresses. AND she’s also a favorite of Oprah which causes controversy on its own level that I don’t care to go into but still am squeamish about…<br /><br />5. Hype. Wasn’t it one of the books nominated for a GR Book of the Year? <br /><br />6. It’s a book that one of my co-workers would look at and say ‘Oh, I’ve read that!’ (a definite sign to stay away)<br /><br />7.It’s set in Masada… and I don’t know about you, but I had no idea what Masada was. Maybe it’s my Titanic, I don’t know. But, going off of #1, I didn’t like it when I mentioned this to a friend and he said ‘Oh, when <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[all the Jews killed themselves and their families rather than be captured by the Romans, what a massacre!! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> and I was only about 200 pages into it and had already started to care for some of the characters and well, HATE IT when surprises are ruined. (run-on sentence, whatever…)and.. well (#1)<br /><br />So, yes, there were many challenges to face reading this book. I’m not even sure why it was on my To-Read list… it’s not like any of you suggested it to me. Or, I think I might have run across this ditty at some point because I had a déjà vu type moment when I read it:<br /><br /><i>“For those who say that the Witch of Moab never loved anyone, that she was selfish, concerned with her own fate alone, I can only say that she was ruined by love and delivered by it and that she left something glorious to the word, a child who loves to stand in the rain.”</i><br /><br />Okay, yes, you’ve written it off as a chick lit book. I get it. I would too based on that.. which is, by the way, on the last page of the novel and couldn’t have ruined my opinion of the story no how. <br /><br />This book is elegant. That’s probably the highest praise that I can bestow right now. I crave to be elegant. I am clumsy and messy and blabber and start sentences with ‘and’ and disregard all my 7th grade English teacher, Mrs. Van Houten, taught me about punctuation. <br /><br />The story is based on actual events, you can even see some of the items mentioned in it at museums and such. But, what Hoffman has done has created characters around this story. The lives of four Dovekeepers and the people who surround them. She presents them to you in such a way that you actually feel like you were handling doves (that is, if you liked them… and c’mon… too many weddings show that the cliché is true.) The women are capable and graceful and intelligent. They have a quiet strength and live with many ghosts. They have all come to Masada for different reasons and fate leads them to one another and we hear each of their stories in their own voice. The Assassin’s Daughter. The Baker’s Wife. The Warrior’s Beloved. The Witch of Moab. <br /><br />Hoffman’s writing style was simple, short sentences. Statements, rather. But, beautifully written, so much so that I would find myself going back to the pages that I marked off to re-read passages. So much said in so little fashion. <i> “I took my hand from his. He looked like ice, but ice is known to burn.” </i> Or <i>“ When the wind is so strong that we women know we will choke on the rising dust if we fail to tie our scarves across our faces, boys will always ignore the elements and race through storm clouds, dreaming of glory.”</i><br /><br />The first page of the book, before the map even, has this written on it: <br /><br /><i>“Let my burden be your burden, and yours be mine.” </i><br /><br />Much better than that crap people recite from that wedding song. Life is hard. People die and people suffer and the most honorable thing you can do is help carry the weight. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13858510911918326645" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_225308471" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/225308471" />
    <a title="Amy Bruno" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1774372-amy-bruno"><img alt="Amy Bruno" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493316012p2/1774372.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/225308471?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy Bruno" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy Bruno" href="/user/show/1774372-amy-bruno">Amy Bruno</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Amy by: </span>
      Sharon Kay Penman
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1774372-amy-bruno?shelf=read-in-2011">read-in-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1774372-amy-bruno?shelf=1st-century">1st-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1774372-amy-bruno?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1774372-amy-bruno?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1774372-amy-bruno?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer225308471" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7484294909780290647">Never before has a book entranced me the way The Dovekeepers by Alice Hoffman has. I’ve literally just finished reading this book and I can’t get my mind to settle down. It’s almost as if I’ve ran a race, my adrenaline is pumping and my heart beating a mile a minute, scenes from the book keep going around and around in my head. My urgent need to tell everyone about this beautifully haunting book is taking over all other thoughts, so I need to get this review out before I explode!<br/><br/>Alice Hoffman is</span>
  <span id="freeText7484294909780290647" style="display:none">Never before has a book entranced me the way The Dovekeepers by Alice Hoffman has. I’ve literally just finished reading this book and I can’t get my mind to settle down. It’s almost as if I’ve ran a race, my adrenaline is pumping and my heart beating a mile a minute, scenes from the book keep going around and around in my head. My urgent need to tell everyone about this beautifully haunting book is taking over all other thoughts, so I need to get this review out before I explode!<br /><br />Alice Hoffman is the author of Practical Magic and The Red Garden, plus many, many other novels but The Dovekeepers is her first historical fiction work. Hoffman was inspired by a trip to Jerusalem, when she visited a place called Masada, a fortress built by Herod the Great that is situated on top of a large mountain, where a group of nine hundred Jews in the first century CE, who had fled their homelands from the invading Romans converged together to escape the slaughter of their people.<br /><br />The Dovekeepers is the story of four women – Aziza, the girl-warrior; Shirah, the Witch of Moab; Yael, the lioness; and Revka, the revenging mother – who came to Masada after their homes and towns were destroyed, each arriving a completely different woman than who they had been before the invasion. They tell the stories of their own life, prior to and including their time in Masada, up to that fateful day and each story is more haunting and profound than the next. <br /><br />Hoffman’s writing is among the most exquisite I have ever read! She has expertly woven a tale of tragedy and hope, hate and love, magic and human nature and I was mesmerized by every word. This book should come with a warning as it has the power to thoroughly enthrall a reader, causing them to block out the world around them completely. My household chores were abandoned, husband and child ignored, I was just absorbed into the pages of this novel that everything else took a backseat to the fascinating story enfolding in front of me. <br /><br />I cried when I finished the novel, not only because of the sad fate of the people of Masada, but because I will miss these four bold and unyielding women. I will carry their story in my heart forever.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7484294909780290647" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1229696636" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1229696636" />
    <a title="Will Byrnes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes"><img alt="Will Byrnes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1291462190p2/1526851.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1229696636?book_show_action=true">Mar 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Will Byrnes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Will Byrnes" href="/user/show/1526851-will-byrnes">Will Byrnes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=books-of-the-year-2011">books-of-the-year-2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1526851-will-byrnes?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1229696636" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10422567685624602566"><blockquote>
  <b>We were no different from the doves above us. We could not speak or cry, but when there was no choice we discovered we could fly. It you want a reason, take this: We yearned for our portion of the sky.</b>
</blockquote>Masada, the word summons up images, war, Romans, Zealots, slaughter, mass suicide. A place of national pride for some, historical and archaeological controversy for many, a bit of Python mockery to others. On visiting the place itself Alice Hoffman was inspired to wonder about the experience of</span>
  <span id="freeText10422567685624602566" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>We were no different from the doves above us. We could not speak or cry, but when there was no choice we discovered we could fly. It you want a reason, take this: We yearned for our portion of the sky.</b>
</blockquote>Masada, the word summons up images, war, Romans, Zealots, slaughter, mass suicide. A place of national pride for some, historical and archaeological controversy for many, a bit of Python mockery to others. On visiting the place itself Alice Hoffman was inspired to wonder about the experience of the women who had lived and died there. The result is <i>The Dovekeepers</i>. She uses the writings of Jewish historian Flavius <a href="http://www.josephus.org/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Josephus</a> as the foundation for her tale. (The Monty Python crew used Josephus’s writings as well, for a very different purpose, in <i>Life of Brian</i>.) <br><br>The four primary characters meet at Masada, where they are assigned to care for the doves. There are those who might consider this a hardship post, regarding doves as <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aL6mTMShVyk" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> dirty, disgusting, filthy, and lice-ridden</a>, or as <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=uvJaqq3eQ8M" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> rats with wings</a>, but they are also a source of fertilizer, meat, eggs, and maybe a bit of <a href="http://www.poetryfoundation.org/poem/171619" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">hope</a>. No one is designated as the concierge. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427385635i/14267453.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Alice Hoffman</b><br><br>The four are Yael, Revka, Aziza, and Shirah. Yael is notable for, among other things, her coloring. Her father, Yosef bar Elhanan, is a notorious assassin, a member of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sicarii" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> Sicarii </a>, a blade-minded branch of the <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zealots_(Judea)" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Zealot</a> movement. They do unpleasant things to Jews who collaborate with the occupying Romans. He was known not only for his effectiveness with sharp objects, but for his talent at going unnoticed. He did notice, however, that his wife died giving birth to their second child, Yael, and, possessing a mind and heart not nearly as honed as his weapons, he blames her. Thanks, Pop. <blockquote>
  <b>All the while I was growing up I wondered what it might be like to have a father who wouldn’t turn away from the sight of me, one who told me I was beautiful, even though my hair flamed a strange red color and my skin was sprinkled with earth-toned flecks as though I’d been splattered with mud. I’d heard my father say to another man that these marks were specks of my mother’s blood.</b>
</blockquote>Their relationship is, shall we say, strained. Big brother, Amram, however, is the apple of papa’s eye, (I know, shocking) even follows him into the family business. That business involves doing in a Roman general, which gains them the attention of the occupying force and the family is forced to beat a hasty exodus from Jerusalem. They team up with another Sicarii family, headed by Jachim ben Simon. Things get complicated. They all endure a trial by heat, sand and misery on their trek, offering witness to others’ tales of sundry Roman atrocities as well. It is a road of self-discovery for Yael, and she arrives at Masada much changed from who she was when she had set out.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427439968i/14270906._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Rachel Broshahan as Yael</b> - from CBS<br><br>Revka had a nice family. Hubby was a baker. Her daughter was married to a nice studious young man. They had two boys. Romans sacked their town, murdering Revka’s husband while slaughtering anyone within reach. Revka is forced to become a refugee. Further atrocities are visited on her family. While she gets a measure of revenge on the latest evil-doers, she darkens her own soul. Her grandchildren have become mute and her nice-young-man of a son-in-law has become a psycho warrior. <br><br>Aziza and her mother were sexually assaulted when Aziza was still a child. Mom decided to raise her as a boy to reduce the likelihood of that happening again. She becomes a bad-ass warrior. Her brother not so much. There is a scene that could have been pulled from Robin Hood in which Aziza demonstrates her proficiency with a bow and arrow. Also gawjuss. Think Xena, at least I did. (you sprouts out there might conjure Katniss) <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427440825i/14270966._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Kathryn Prescott as Aziza</b> - from CBS<br><br>Last and definitely not least is Shirah. A witchy sort, with a book of magic spells, great hair and ravishing beauty. She comes from a line of women in a particular line of work, but her mother sent her away from their home in Alexandria when she was young, as an anti-them pogrom was going on, to stay with relations in Jerusalem. Things do not go well for her there. She meets The One, but there is a mess with him being already married, and not up to standing up to his parents, and her being, oh, twelve. She later finds someone with whom to share a home, pops out a few progeny, but is now a single mom in Masada, doing the odd spell to help female residents with this and that, and still looking up to the goddess <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astarte" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Ashtoreth</a> for her main religious sustenance. But what’s the deal with her and the hunky head of the Masada warriors, Eleazar Ben Ya'ir? And what’s up with his seriously creepy wife?<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1427441103i/14270968._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="xxx" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><b>Cote de Pablo as Shirah</b> - from CBS<br><br>So that’s the four. We know (you know, right?) that things do not go well for the residents of Club Masada. The story is in tracking the progress of the place’s demise and how the four got there, and how they cope with the stresses that are steadily building. We are also given a bit of a tour, and get a sense of place beyond the stick figure general notion.<br><br>Hoffman definitely has an inclination towards incorporating history into her work, whether of the maritime sort in <i>Blackbird House</i> or a bit of Transcendentalism in <i>The Red Garden</i>. She is also fond of incorporating dollops of magic into her tales, sometimes more than a little. She usually tells tales of women who are forced to cope with challenging circumstances. And she is quite fond of fairy tales. It will come as no shock that this novel is very much in keeping with her previous work. What makes it different is its ambition, scope, and length. It is not a huge book, at 500 pages or so, but is bulkier than her previous work.<br><br>First, and probably most important, it is an engaging read. Her main characters are interesting, all strong in their way, and worth finding out about. The story moves along at a decent pace, most of the time. Place is of obviously central import and is given star treatment. I would not say that you could matter-transmit yourself to the fort and know your way around, but you might see places that look familiar and wonder how you knew about them. Hoffman mixes martial material of different flavors, blending some warriors in combat with the more appalling laying waste of defenseless civilians by armed sorts from both sides. There is romantic entanglement aplenty, but my guy-genes did not feel much inclination to generate spew. It all worked pretty well. <br><br>She may have overdone it a bit with her imagery, IMHO. Yael, in particular, is associated with, among other things, a Flaming Tree image. Red hair, get it? There are other bits of significance associated with this, but it seemed to me that it was popping up like one of those birthday candles that won’t go out. Yael is also associated with lions, in various guises, a love interest, an encounter with a feline or two in the desert, a kittie held captive by the occupying army. As a host to six of the creatures, I know that, however much we may love and be fascinated by them, sometimes you need to step back a bit. Maybe it is just that in a longer book there are more mentions than one is used to from Hoffman, who knows her way around imagery. I do not recall feeling bugged by other such strands. Watch for image streams relating to serpents and boids, sorry, birds (I <i>am</i> from Brooklyn, after all) Hoffman associates some elemental aspects with her characters, which seemed very fairy-tale-ish and ok. Shirah is associated with water, for example, and that aspect was used in moderation and worked quite well. <br><br>Magic most definitely plays a part here. Spells are cast and have the expected impact. Of course some of what works is an expert’s knowledge of science, and that <i>seems like</i> magic at times. It is suggested that one character’s cloak has a feature may make it a likely ancestor of a similar garment used in Hogwarts. One expects magic in AH’s novels. This is all good. <br><br>For her historical basis, Hoffman relies on the writings of Flavius Josephus. Here we get into a bit of controversy. The tale of mass suicide that is Masada appears not to have a particularly strong foundation in archaeological research. It was fluffed at a time when it served well as a symbol of Israeli determination and nationhood. Evidence that proves that the events Josephus describes actually occurred is less than entirely persuasive. While there are certainly elements of Josephus’s tale that have a basis in reality, others might constitute a bit of playing to his audience. We all have our national myths. Think George Washington and the Cherry Tree, Paul Revere’s ride, WMDs in Iraq. I do not fault Hoffman for centering her tale around a historical event that is less than universally accepted. Myth is what she does. And she has done an outstanding job with this one. Whether one sees the source material as ancient history or a mythologization of a less exceptional reality, the story she spins around that core is a compelling one. <br><br>I have only read a handful of Alice Hoffman’s adult books, so cannot claim a deep knowledge of her oeuvre. But I would put my shekels on <i>The Dovekeepers</i> being the crowning achievement of her career. (One might say it is the feather in her literary cap. I wouldn’t, but some might.)<br><br>Review posted – 3/28/15<br><br>Pub Date – 10/4/11<br><br>This review is also posted at <a href="http://wp.me/p3I01S-kX" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Cootsreviews.com</a><br><br>=============================<b>EXTRA STUFF</b><br><br>Links to the author’s <a href="http://alicehoffman.com" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">personal</a> and <a href="https://www.facebook.com/AliceHoffmanAuthor" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">FB</a> pages<br><br>Here is a <a href="http://alicehoffman.com/books/the-dovekeepers/reading-guide/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> reading guide</a> from Hoffman’s site<br><br>The CBS <a href="http://www.cbs.com/shows/the-dovekeepers/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">mini-series</a> is due any day now. The series makes do with three of the four primary characters, (sorry Revka) and Josephus is not a character in the book. <br><br>Oy, there are so many unfamiliar words used in this story that it would be a useful thing to have kept track of them. Sorry, kids, I did not. However, AH does collect some of those in a <a href="http://alicehoffman.com/books/the-dovekeepers/glossary/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">glossary</a> on her site. It is not comprehensive, though. There are plenty more in the book.<br><br>A documentary that looks at the historical event: <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=m49AnhpMBdg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener"> Time Travellers: Myth of Masada</a><br><br>Here is a nifty site if you are interested in this particular sort of <s>boid</s> <a href="http://beautyofbirds.com/doves.htm" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">bird</a><br><br>A couple of <b>songs</b> that seem, vaguely, suitable<br><br> Yes, yes, I know the title of the song is Edge of Seventeen, but I imagine most of us think of it as <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ykam-0DfKA4" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">The White Winged Dove</a><br><br> A favorite from a non-Jewish <a href="http://video.naij.com/view=6qgucczw&amp;ref=search_redirect&amp;autoplay=1" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Prince</a><br><br>And then there is <b>Monty Python</b>, noted at the top. <a href="http://freethoughtblogs.com/singham/2014/09/07/a-monty-pythons-life-of-brian-puzzle-explained/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">Here</a> is a site that not only links to the infamous Python suicide scene from <i>Life of Brian</i>, but offers a look at a scene, cut from the film, that had been intended to set it up.<br><br><b>Other Hoffman books I have reviewed</b>:<br>-----<a <div id="review_254923514" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/254923514" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811208-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597195013p2/811208.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/254923514?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/811208-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811208-anna?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811208-anna?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811208-anna?shelf=witchcraft-and-wizardry">witchcraft-and-wizardry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/811208-anna?shelf=based-on-a-true-story">based-on-a-true-story</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer254923514" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6624014488605090790"><i>The Dovekeepers</i> on its surface sounds like it has the potential to be incredibly moving, and I had high hopes. Yet I found the reading to be more of an exercise in frustration and perseverance. The frustration is largely the result of the book's lack of development, which might sound weird to say about a 500+ page novel. It is described on its cover as a story of the siege of Masada, but fans of historical fiction will be sadly disappointed; the historical context is woefully underdeveloped. It </span>
  <span id="freeText6624014488605090790" style="display:none"><i>The Dovekeepers</i> on its surface sounds like it has the potential to be incredibly moving, and I had high hopes. Yet I found the reading to be more of an exercise in frustration and perseverance. The frustration is largely the result of the book's lack of development, which might sound weird to say about a 500+ page novel. It is described on its cover as a story of the siege of Masada, but fans of historical fiction will be sadly disappointed; the historical context is woefully underdeveloped. It happens on the fringes of the women's lives. Fans of fantasy will find the development of the magic weak. I wondered why Hoffman decided to include the magical aspect if she wasn't going to fully commit to it. Finally, fans of strong women protagonists will be seriously pissed off by how flat and unidimensional most of the women come off as. Which brings me to perseverance. The first section of the novel feels like you are trapped in wandering the dessert with the characters, and not it a "oh the description is so vivid I feel like I'm there" way but in a "dear god when will this end" way. I would have given up if I didn't have to finish this for my book club. I wish I could say that the story picks up, but for me it continued to be really uneven with section that bogged down the story.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6624014488605090790" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer16162577143746472585">The Good Stuff<br/><br/> * I wish I had the words to express how wonderful this book is. I will be honest if Simon and Schuster hadn't sent it to me for review, I probably wouldn't have picked it up and let me tell you that would have been a shame. This book is haunting and sad but yet so full of hope and of the resilience of the human spirit<br/> * Beautiful raw and honest story and just so god-damned emotional to read<br/> * Exceptionally real and strong female characters<br/> * History written so it comes alive and y</span>
  <span id="freeText16162577143746472585" style="display:none">The Good Stuff<br /><br /> * I wish I had the words to express how wonderful this book is. I will be honest if Simon and Schuster hadn't sent it to me for review, I probably wouldn't have picked it up and let me tell you that would have been a shame. This book is haunting and sad but yet so full of hope and of the resilience of the human spirit<br /> * Beautiful raw and honest story and just so god-damned emotional to read<br /> * Exceptionally real and strong female characters<br /> * History written so it comes alive and you learn so much without feeling like you are getting a history lesson<br /> * The faults/flaws of the characters are not hidden and you see how they change and grow over the course of the story<br /> * Emotionally raw by the end of the story and had to go hug my children before I could go to bed<br /> * Obviously thoroughly researched and you feel the authors passion for the subject matter as it never comes across as dull<br /> * I would be shocked if this doesn't get made into a movie or a mini series<br /> * Further Reading at the end of the book is appreciated for those like me who will want to find out more of the history<br /> * Very wise and thought provoking<br /> * A wonderful book for various discussions about faith, forgiveness, compassion, woman's rights etc<br /> * Why are you still reading my review -- get thee to a book store now and buy it -- hello what are you waiting for<br /><br />The Not so Good Stuff<br /><br /> * Could have been perfect with a some stronger editing. There is some obvious repetition that should have been caught and it would have made it a truly brilliant novel.<br /> * I was forced to stay up till a 1am to finish this and the last 15 pages or so I could barely read with the tears falling down. Alice you owe me some coffee and Kleenex &amp; an apology to my kids for mommy being cranky from lack of sleep<br /><br />Favorite Quotes/Passages<br /><br /><br />"Try as she might to keep him a child, Shirah's son was already straining to be a man. She called out cautions, but Adir hurried to the garrison, determined to be among the men he admired. When the wind is so strong that we women know we will choke on the rising dust if we fail to tie our scarves across our faces, boys will always ignore the elements and race through storm clouds, dreaming of glory. Even a witch can't stop her son from becoming a warrior. There is no spell great enough for that."<br /><br /><br />"It was sometimes easier to be with a stranger from whom nothing was expected and to whom nothing was granted in return."<br /><br /><br />"They embraced the feminine aspect of God, the Dwelling. the deep place where inspiration abided, for in the written words of God, compassion and knowledge were always female."<br /><br /><br />"The desire for Jerusalem was a fire that could not be quenched. There was a spark inside the holiest of holy places that made people want to possess it, and what men yearn for they often destroy."<br /><br /><br />What I Learned<br /><br /> * Man it really sucked to be a women in ancient times, we are so lucky in this day and age to be treated, for the most part, as the equals that we are (still so far to go)<br /> * Now I really already know this, but lets just put this out again -- the persecution of Jews over and over again just completely baffles me. They truly are one of the most formidable, strong and resilient race the world has ever seen.<br /> * Tons of fascinating information about 70-75 CE<br /><br /> Who should/shouldn't read<br /><br /> * Will be buying a copy for my niece, sister and sister in laws for Christmas because I don't want to lend them MY copy and risk the chance of one of them --- "misplacing it" LOL<br /> * Those who enjoy a nice light read, would probably not want to pick one up. It is quite intense and detailed <br /> * Thinking those of Roman descent might be a little put off<br /> * A must have for public libraries<br /><br />4.75 Dewey's<br /><br /><br />I received this from Simon and Schuster in exchange for an honest review -- thank you for once again breaking me out of my comfort zone and introducing me to something so spectacular</span>

<div id="review_365345977" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/365345977" />
    <a title="Linda Hart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2455252-linda-hart"><img alt="Linda Hart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1549256566p2/2455252.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/365345977?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Linda Hart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Linda Hart" href="/user/show/2455252-linda-hart">Linda Hart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer365345977" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11636008878562328380">An exceptional read. This is a masterpiece, a wonderful, mesmerizing book about the lives of four strong Jewish women, each with different backgrounds, powerful personalities, and distinct stories of there own. Their histories collided when destiny/circumstances found them living at the Jewish stronghold, Masada, during the last and final siege by the Romans. According to the historian, Josephus, two of these women and five children survived the horrendous event. <br/><br/>I agree with Janet, a goodreads </span>
  <span id="freeText11636008878562328380" style="display:none">An exceptional read. This is a masterpiece, a wonderful, mesmerizing book about the lives of four strong Jewish women, each with different backgrounds, powerful personalities, and distinct stories of there own. Their histories collided when destiny/circumstances found them living at the Jewish stronghold, Masada, during the last and final siege by the Romans. According to the historian, Josephus, two of these women and five children survived the horrendous event. <br /><br />I agree with Janet, a goodreads reviewer who said the following: <i>Words can be spoken or written. This book did for me what I love about books---it used the written word to create a world for me as real as the one I'm living in now.</i><br /><br />On Oct 03, 2011, Another goodreads reviewer, Jennifer Rayment, wrote such an excellent review that I am quoting it in entirety here:<br /><br /><i>The Good Stuff<br /><br />* I wish I had the words to express how wonderful this book is. I will be honest if Simon and Schuster hadn't sent it to me for review, I probably wouldn't have picked it up and let me tell you that would have been a shame. This book is haunting and sad but yet so full of hope and of the resilience of the human spirit<br />* Beautiful raw and honest story and just so god-damned emotional to read<br />* Exceptionally real and strong female characters<br />* History written so it comes alive and you learn so much without feeling like you are getting a history lesson<br />* The faults/flaws of the characters are not hidden and you see how they change and grow over the course of the story<br />* Emotionally raw by the end of the story and had to go hug my children before I could go to bed<br />* Obviously thoroughly researched and you feel the authors passion for the subject matter as it never comes across as dull<br />* I would be shocked if this doesn't get made into a movie or a mini series<br />* Further Reading at the end of the book is appreciated for those like me who will want to find out more of the history<br />* Very wise and thought provoking<br />* A wonderful book for various discussions about faith, forgiveness, compassion, woman's rights etc<br />* Why are you still reading my review -- get thee to a book store now and buy it -- hello what are you waiting for<br /><br />The Not so Good Stuff<br /><br />* Could have been perfect with a some stronger editing. There is some obvious repetition that should have been caught and it would have made it a truly brilliant novel.<br />* I was forced to stay up till a 1am to finish this and the last 15 pages or so I could barely read with the tears falling down. Alice you owe me some coffee and Kleenex &amp; an apology to my kids for mommy being cranky from lack of sleep<br /><br />What I Learned<br /><br />* Man it really sucked to be a women in ancient times, we are so lucky in this day and age to be treated, for the most part, as the equals that we are (still so far to go)<br />* Now I really already know this, but lets just put this out again -- the persecution of Jews over and over again just completely baffles me. They truly are one of the most formidable, strong and resilient race the world has ever seen.<br />* Tons of fascinating information about 70-75 CE<br /><br />Who should/shouldn't read<br /><br />* Will be buying a copy for my niece, sister and sister in laws for Christmas because I don't want to lend them MY copy and risk the chance of one of them --- "misplacing it" LOL<br />* Those who enjoy a nice light read, would probably not want to pick one up. It is quite intense and detailed <br />* Thinking those of Roman descent might be a little put off<br />* A must have for public libraries</i><br /><br />I listened to the audio version, which is very well done with four different women doing the narrative reading of the four main characters. If it were possible I would give this book 6 stars. It is among the best books I've read in a long time and touched me deeply.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11636008878562328380" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_200931070" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/200931070" />
    <a title="Janet" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3851272-janet"><img alt="Janet" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1279153317p2/3851272.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/200931070?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Janet" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Janet" href="/user/show/3851272-janet">Janet</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3851272-janet?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3851272-janet?shelf=book-club-pick">book-club-pick</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3851272-janet?shelf=2011-libfavs">2011-libfavs</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3851272-janet?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer200931070" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9155041179407268135">For fans of Alice Hoffman this book is a mixture of the new and the familiar. Devotees will recognize the strong women with unusual, even magical abilities. What's new is the historical setting and time---the fortress of Masada in the year 70 CE. Also, Hoffman's language struck me as different in this book. <br/><br/>I think whether or not readers like this book will hinge on their reaction to the language Hoffman uses to recreate the world of her women. I loved the level of detail. I felt the sea salt in</span>
  <span id="freeText9155041179407268135" style="display:none">For fans of Alice Hoffman this book is a mixture of the new and the familiar. Devotees will recognize the strong women with unusual, even magical abilities. What's new is the historical setting and time---the fortress of Masada in the year 70 CE. Also, Hoffman's language struck me as different in this book. <br /><br />I think whether or not readers like this book will hinge on their reaction to the language Hoffman uses to recreate the world of her women. I loved the level of detail. I felt the sea salt in my hair, the rocks pricking my bare feet, the sun beating mercilessly on my head. I tasted the saltiness of the olive crop. I realized how central religious beliefs were to how the women lived their daily lives, and how gender affected their choices. I understood when they chose to break the rules and empathized at the price they paid. <br /><br />One of the themes of this story is the value of words; their meaning and use are important to the characters. Words are cited as the first thing created by God. Hoffman uses this novel to give words to the women of the time, to let their voices be heard. <br /><br />Words can be spoken or written. This book did for me what I love about books---it used the written word to create a world for me as real as the one I'm living in now. <br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="9155041179407268135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9873053116156444413">Alice Hoffman brought me back in time and I enjoyed learning about what life was like in 70 C.E. The writing was beautiful and at times poetic. I like my poetry in short doses though and after two hundred pages of lyrical prose I was ready to move on with the story. I liked the book, in fact I was swept away in the lives of the brave women it was about. Revka, Aziza, Shirah and Yael were strong women who lived amazing lives and were the only survivors of the Masada massacre. Unfortunately, the p</span>
  <span id="freeText9873053116156444413" style="display:none">Alice Hoffman brought me back in time and I enjoyed learning about what life was like in 70 C.E. The writing was beautiful and at times poetic. I like my poetry in short doses though and after two hundred pages of lyrical prose I was ready to move on with the story. I liked the book, in fact I was swept away in the lives of the brave women it was about. Revka, Aziza, Shirah and Yael were strong women who lived amazing lives and were the only survivors of the Masada massacre. Unfortunately, the parts of the story that I first found to be so interesting started to bore me. The amount of detail the author put into describing religious beliefs and rituals made the story drag. I felt like I only needed to read about the steps to prepare one's body for death only once. Instead, the author felt like she had to pound the information into my skull again and again. After a while that is how I felt.... like the author was pounding me upside the head with her overly written book. Death by poetry. I started to skim over the pages looking for the meat of the story till I found the end. I'm glad I finished it.<br><br>Pictures I found relating to the story:<br><br>Masada<br><a href="http://www.flickr.com/photos/61266707@N07/8206271468/" title="Masada by chrisymarie1, on Flickr" rel="nofollow noopener">

<div id="review_3644454766" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3644454766" />
    <a title="Dem" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2752994-dem"><img alt="Dem" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1552868566p2/2752994.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3644454766?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dem" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dem" href="/user/show/2752994-dem">Dem</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3644454766" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12874846991821470550"><b>
  <i> I liked it and might even have loved it had it been shortened by 100 pages</i>
</b>  <br/><br/><br/>A fascinating period of history impeccably researched and tightly woven into an interesting tale taking the reader back in time to the first century Masada an ancient mountaintop fortress in southeastern Israel. The site of the Jews’ last stand against the Romans after the fall of Jerusalem in 70 CE. Nine hundred Jews held out for months against armies of Romans. Two women and five children survived to tell the tale</span>
  <span id="freeText12874846991821470550" style="display:none"><b>
  <i> I liked it and might even have loved it had it been shortened by 100 pages</i>
</b>  <br><br><br>A fascinating period of history impeccably researched and tightly woven into an interesting tale taking the reader back in time to the first century Masada an ancient mountaintop fortress in southeastern Israel. The site of the Jews’ last stand against the Romans after the fall of Jerusalem in 70 CE. Nine hundred Jews held out for months against armies of Romans. Two women and five children survived to tell the tale and the Dove keepers brings the tale to life. <br><br>I am always intrigued and fascinated when events in history come alive in historical fiction. There is no doubt that when Alice Hoffman set out to write this novel she really took her research very seriously. It is clear she wanted to give the her characters a voice and retell this ancient story to a new generation of readers. In doing so she educates and entertains with this memorable fictionalised story based on a historical event. <br><br>I know I have reacted differently than a lot of my Goodread friends with The Dovekeepers. I enjoyed the beautiful prose and vivid setting to begin with but to be honest by the end of the book I grew slightly tired and felt the story dragged on. (and this is just my opinion). The characters were a little difficult to distinguish between and on picking up the novel every evening it took me a little while to settle back into the story and connect with the characters. As with Hoffman’s books there is always a magical element to her stories and in this one it felt a little overdone or forced and even unexplained. While I loved some of her other books I liked this one, I just didn't love it hence my 3 star rating. <br><br>Having said that I enjoyed the book and it was a worthwhile read. I was lucky to have both the audio and hard copy. The audio is beautifully narrated and switching between the two really worked for me. I wouldn't want to dissuade any readers from reading this novel, as the writing is beautiful, the historical content fascinating. I am so glad to finally have added this one to my read shelf. <br><br>I think readers who enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4989.The_Red_Tent" title="The Red Tent by Anita Diamant" rel="nofollow noopener">The Red Tent</a><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4989.The_Red_Tent" title="The Red Tent by Anita Diamant" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/books/1405739117l/4989._SY75_.jpg" alt="The Red Tent by Anita Diamant" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> may love this one too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12874846991821470550" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_211192109" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/211192109" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/211192109?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11118511-the-dovekeepers">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=israel">israel</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer211192109" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7814673134871671685">4.5 Stars<br/><br/><i>”We came like doves across the desert. In a time when there was nothing but death, we were grateful for anything, and most grateful of all when we awoke to another day.”</i><br/><i>
  <b>”We had been wandering for so long I forgot what it was like to live within walls or sleep through the night. In that time I lost all I might have possessed if Jerusalem had not fallen: a husband, a family, a future of my own. My girlhood disappeared in the desert. The person I’d once been vanished as I wrapped myself</b></i></span>
  <span id="freeText7814673134871671685" style="display:none">4.5 Stars<br /><br /><i>”We came like doves across the desert. In a time when there was nothing but death, we were grateful for anything, and most grateful of all when we awoke to another day.”</i><br /><i>
  <b>”We had been wandering for so long I forgot what it was like to live within walls or sleep through the night. In that time I lost all I might have possessed if Jerusalem had not fallen: a husband, a family, a future of my own. My girlhood disappeared in the desert. The person I’d once been vanished as I wrapped myself in white when the dust rose in clouds. We were nomads, leaving behind beds and belongings, rugs and brass pots. Now our house was the house of the desert, black at night, brutally white at noon.<br /><br />“They say the truest beauty is in the harshest land and that God can be found there by those with open eyes.”</b>
</i><br /><br />The stories of four different women eventually merge, as this begins in 70 CE, with the story of Yael – the daughter of Yosef bar Elhanan, an assassin associated with the Sicarii, a splinter group of the Jewish Zealots. Yael’s mother had died in giving birth to her, for which her father blames Yael. Still, when they flee Jerusalem after the destruction of the Temple, her father takes Yael with him, traveling with Jachim ben Simon, another assassin along with the members of his family.<br /><br /><i>
  <b>”Everywhere I walked my fate walked with me, sewn to my feet with red thread. All that will ever be has already been written long before it happens.”</b>
</i><br /><br />Revka is the wife of the Baker, and her story begins as her husband, a good and pious man, has left loaves in the oven and is off with the men while Revka is just beginning her day with her two grandsons, when their world is shattered. <br /><br /><i>”Blessed is He who spoke, and the world came into being. <b>Just as creation began with words so, too, did our world come apart in silence. None of us spoke. The boys because they could not, my son-in-law because he would not, myself because there were no words worth speaking aloud. The world was broken, and there was only one road that remained, splayed open before us as if made of bones.”</b></i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>”We had no choice but to go forward, as only emptiness was around us. The following day we did so. I had to leave that unmarked place, abandoning the last of my husband’s essence. I carried my loss as my burden; it weighed me down and made me slow. I could not keep peace with the tired donkeys who bleakly made their way. The boys ran back to me and grabbed my hands and urged me on. Because of them I continued, but God must have known it had crossed my mind to stay behind. I wanted to lie down beside the rocks and dream of the Baker, to call for him to come back to me, even if it meant giving up with world. Perhaps that was the sin I committed. I forgot that even the worst of lives is a treasure.”</b>
</i><br /><br />Revka and Yael both arrive in Masada, a fortress set on a mountaintop. There they both come to work in the dovecote, collecting eggs and distributing the compost they gather. Among those in Masada are Shirah and her daughters, one of which is named Aziza, the other Nahara.<br /><br />Aziza was a daughter cherished by her mother, and loved by a father figure that taught her how to protect herself, to be capable of fighting to defend herself, her sister and mother. <br /><br /><i>
  <b>”But no matter how you might bow before others, my sister, the bond between us will last all eternity, until we meet again in a place where nothing can separate us, as it was on the night you were born, in your father’s tent, with my breath inside you and my life the thread that kept you in this world.”</b>
</i><br /><br />The fourth story belongs to Shirah, where we learn of her youth as a beloved and privileged child, a daughter of a consort of the high priests, a woman who studies medicine, spells, and the powers of amulets and charms – a keshaphim, a woman tied to Shechinah, the feminine characteristic of God. A woman taught everything by her mother about this world, everything necessary to carrying into the World-to-Come. She knew the cure for a scorpion bite, that the <i>“nectar of the spiky blue flower of the hyssop dabbed on the wrist would ward off evil,</i> a woman who wore the tooth of a black dog around her neck for protection from wild beasts, reciting incantations when digging for the roots of henbane, a holy plant. <br /><br /><i>
  <b>”Inside the locked box was a notebook of parchment upon which my mother had written the many secrets she had accumulated over the years. It was a recipe book for the human heart, for our people believe that all we know and all we have experience is contained there.”</b>
</i><br /><br /><i>
  <b>”We were no different from the doves <br />above us.<br />We could not speak or cry, but when <br />there was<br />no choice we discovered we could fly. If<br />you<br />want a reason, take this: We yearned <br />for our <br />portion of the sky.”</b>
</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="7814673134871671685" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1490144311" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1490144311" />
    <a title="Sean Barrs " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs"><img alt="Sean Barrs " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605281953p2/27788046.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1490144311?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Barrs " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Barrs " href="/user/show/27788046-sean-barrs">Sean Barrs </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=1-star-reads">1-star-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=ancient-rome">ancient-rome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/27788046-sean-barrs?shelf=love-and-romance">love-and-romance</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1490144311" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5162073446669390670">This was <u>massively underwhelming.</u> The plot was terrible; it had no real development or drive. In reality, it barely existed at all. The story just followed the lives of some rather <u>mundane characters.</u> There was little to keep me reading. They weren’t really striving for anything, beyond survival; thus, I found myself getting quite bored with their stories. They just weren’t very remarkable. The historical detail was minimal, and the random magic elements that were worked in the plot were just so</span>
  <span id="freeText5162073446669390670" style="display:none">This was <u>massively underwhelming.</u> The plot was terrible; it had no real development or drive. In reality, it barely existed at all. The story just followed the lives of some rather <u>mundane characters.</u> There was little to keep me reading. They weren’t really striving for anything, beyond survival; thus, I found myself getting quite bored with their stories. They just weren’t very remarkable. The historical detail was minimal, and the random magic elements that were worked in the plot were just so <u>pointless</u> that they might as well have been taken out. <br><br><b>I was rather annoyed.</b><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451725154ra/17588755.gif" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I would even go as far to say that this wasn’t really historical fiction. Well, at least not in the traditional sense. The author has evoked the era well in terms of the social aspects she portrayed. The Roman Empire’s brutality can be seen in the stories; they are ignorant to ways that are not their own. They are quick to supress and control. But, in terms of the huge political events, the ones that change governments and shape nations, this was hugely underdeveloped. It was barely portrayed at all; it wasn’t the focus of the story, the characters were. So, it remained forever in the background. This was more a chronicling of their lives rather than a portrayal of real historical detail. As a huge fan of historical fiction, I was incomparably disappointed because there was so little history involved. The cover was very missleading. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1395106803ra/8947244.gif" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The prose was heavily descriptive. It was massively convoluted in this. The desert scenes were painstakingly arduous in their perpetuity. They just didn’t seem to end. The dessert was clearly suggestive of the character’s situation; they were hopeless and despairing like the far reaching dry dunes. This is great, though I don’t need to be bombarded with the constant comparison. Then, at one point, she gives a ridiculously long section over to describing the wind. I mean the…wind. Great…. I get it. It’s the fucking wind. This needed some elements of subtlety in the writing, and a stripping back of its overworked imagery. <br><br>I’d just had enough of hearing about the desert, and it’s amazingly exciting wind, come the tenth time it was described. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1451725154ra/17588756.gif" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Then there are the characters themselves. I hated them. I hated them all, every last one of them. Well, except for the goat in the first part. I quite liked the goat. That says it all. When the goat is your favourite character in the novel, then you know that you hate it. Not that I’m against goats, but when I read a book I hope for something more than a goat that I can sympathise with. She did have it tough though, I mean, I wouldn’t have wanted to be dragged along with Hoffman’s characters. Well, that kind of happened when I read this excruciating novel, but not in the literal sense. Pity that goat my friends, pity her. <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1451730937i/17590023._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="200" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="5162073446669390670" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1500624496" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1500624496" />
    <a title="Gary" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51196399-gary"><img alt="Gary" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1597783321p2/51196399.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1500624496?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gary" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gary" href="/user/show/51196399-gary">Gary</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1500624496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17676711575969255086">Masada was a historic Jewish fortress that stood on huge rock in Judea (now southern Israel) In 73 CE 960 Jewish patriots killed themselves as Masada rather then surrender to Roman forces. For many Israelis their devotion to liberty is a national symbol called the spirit of Masada. This is a fictional story of four women who may have lived there.<br/><br/>I was thoroughly entranced by this tour de force-a definite masterpiece of 21st century literature. This brilliant novel takes us back to Ancient Israel</span>
  <span id="freeText17676711575969255086" style="display:none">Masada was a historic Jewish fortress that stood on huge rock in Judea (now southern Israel) In 73 CE 960 Jewish patriots killed themselves as Masada rather then surrender to Roman forces. For many Israelis their devotion to liberty is a national symbol called the spirit of Masada. This is a fictional story of four women who may have lived there.<br /><br />I was thoroughly entranced by this tour de force-a definite masterpiece of 21st century literature. This brilliant novel takes us back to Ancient Israel in the first century CE and the lives of four beautiful, sensual, strong and valiant women. Each of whose story interwines with that of the other, and how they came to the fortress of Masada in Israel, commanded by the courageous warrior and remarkable leader of men, Yair Ben Eliezer. I fell in love with all the four women featured in the novel. The strength, courage and wisdom of these women was brilliant and inspirational. A historic novel, a novel of magic, mystery and the spiritual, and sensually written piece of literature about women and exploring the time in history from the viewpoint of women but never a Novel only for women.<br /><br />Yael the daughter of an assassin who'se father treats her with unforgivable cruelty and whose passions and great capacity for love stay with us<br />Revka who survives the horrific rape and murder of her daughter at the hands of Roman soldiers and lives to refind meaning in her life<br /><br />Aziza the girl warrior, brought up as a son, lithe , athletic and beautiful, her fate is to fall in love with a fellow warrior<br /><br />and<br />Shirah, the enchanting , beautiful sorceress, cast out because of bigotry and religious dogma, her fate is to be the great love of the commander of Masada, the great Yair Ben eliezer and who does all she can to save her doomed children.<br /><br />A book of great emotion and haunting as it is exquisitely erotic and evocative of the Land and people of Israel. It teaches something of the time and the place but never lingers on the teaching. Whatever genre you enjoy if good writing and an unforgettable spellbinding narrative is what you are looking for, this book is a must read. I finished it in three days.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17676711575969255086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1288390547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1288390547" />
    <a title="Diane Wallace" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/43342511-diane-wallace"><img alt="Diane Wallace" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1589119509p2/43342511.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1288390547?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diane Wallace" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diane Wallace" href="/user/show/43342511-diane-wallace">Diane Wallace</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer832854751" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer10902791327781754197"><b>2.75★</b><br/>Being a huge AH fan I’m somewhat embarrassed with my rating and reception to this novel. With only a couple of exceptions, every GR friend who read it rated it very highly. Writers Toni Morrison and Wally Lamb praised it as well. So what’s up with me? I offer up my thoughts for those very few of you who also struggled.<br/><br/>I ended up feeling like many of the Jews wandering in the desert for forty years, except for me it was forty years of five hundred pages. Initially, the chosen people were hap</span>
  <span id="freeText10902791327781754197" style="display:none"><b>2.75★</b><br />Being a huge AH fan I’m somewhat embarrassed with my rating and reception to this novel. With only a couple of exceptions, every GR friend who read it rated it very highly. Writers Toni Morrison and Wally Lamb praised it as well. So what’s up with me? I offer up my thoughts for those very few of you who also struggled.<br /><br />I ended up feeling like many of the Jews wandering in the desert for forty years, except for me it was forty years of five hundred pages. Initially, the chosen people were happy to be free of the yokes of slavery and have enough nutritious manna to eat. Then the years did not fly by and the grumbling started in—Are we there yet? Is there anything else to eat besides this white stuff? We’re sick of it. We want meat! I was definitely feeling very ungrateful and unappreciative by the halfway mark.<br />While the author’s prose usually works its magic on me, in this case it was overwrought in my opinion. I think it got it its own way. Weaving a tale around the personal stories of four women and how they came to be at Masada was promising fiction. However they all spoke with the same esoterically flowered speech to the point of reading exhaustion. Since the ending of this historical event was known and would be no surprise reveal, it was all about the journey getting there. It became necessary for me to break and read other books in order to finish. There wasn’t enough meat (and I’m a vegetarian).<br /><br />As stated I’m a fan of the author, so perhaps I need literary counseling? A just okay read for me but it finished well and I am compelled out of loyalty to bump it up to three stars.<br />Thank you dear book family for allowing me to release my reader’s frustration from the very small pond in which I dwell as an outcast. Selah.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10902791327781754197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Carole" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8276857-carole"><img alt="Carole" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/371852626?book_show_action=true">Jul 18, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carole" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carole" href="/user/show/8276857-carole">Carole</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8276857-carole?shelf=books-read-in-2012">books-read-in-2012</a>
